            interstitial: googletag.sizeMapping()

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/adfm.202404797')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="d94e31f40d7a4579b5dcf9f99c85970e">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '49d7e6a3-4c83-4db2-8a59-54f10a0029ed'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

                     <p>Center for Photonics Information and Energy Materials, Shenzhen Institutes of Advanced Technology, Chinese Academy of Sciences, Shenzhen, 518055 P. R. China</p>
                     <p>Department of Microstructure and Residual Stress Analysis, Helmholtz-Zentrum Berlin für Materialien und Energie, Hahn-Meitner-Platz 1, 14109 Berlin, Germany</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Ning/De">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Li/Qingyuan" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a8" data-db-target-for="a8" aria-controls="a8" aria-haspopup="true" id="a8_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Qingyuan Li</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a8" aria-labelledby="a8_Ctrl" role="region" id="a8">
                     <p class="author-name">Qingyuan Li</p>
                     <p>Center of Materials Science and Optoelectronics Engineering, College of Materials Science and Optoelectronic Technology, University of Chinese Academy of Sciences, Beijing, 100049 P. R. China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Li/Qingyuan">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Zhang/Jicheng" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a9" data-db-target-for="a9" aria-controls="a9" aria-haspopup="true" id="a9_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Jicheng Zhang</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a9" aria-labelledby="a9_Ctrl" role="region" id="a9">

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d261997671" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>How to tune the activity and reversibility of oxygen anion redox (OAR) is a critical issue for O3-type sodium-ion battery (SIB) cathodes. Herein, the key role of electronegativity-difference configuration on the activation of OAR is find out, and further tune electronegativity-difference configuration with La incorporation to construct non-bonded O 2<i>p</i> orbitals and achieve the reversible anionic redox in O3-type NaMn<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. Owing to the special extranuclear electronic structure of La<sup>3+</sup> [Xe], the La electron cloud is difficult to be disturbed by the O electron cloud, and some O electrons do not participate in the formation of ionic bonds, thus retaining the non-bonded electrons of O 2<i>p</i> and activating OAR. Moreover, La<sup>3+</sup> doping also decreases the Coulomb force between Na<sup>+</sup> and O<sup>2−</sup> favoring Na<sup>+</sup> migration as well as strengthening the La─O bonds inhibiting the irreversible phase transition. La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coating layer also plays a role on inhibiting the reaction between molecular oxygen and the electrolyte, and making OAR reversible. After modification, the cycling stability is significantly improved (86.9% vs 27.3%@2C@200cycles; 90.8% vs 52.9%@5C@300 cycles). This study presents some insights on OAR activation mechanism and offers a facile strategy to improve the activity and reversibility of OAR for designing high performance SIBs cathodes.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202404797-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202404797-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have been widely applied in many fields due to their higher energy density and good cycling performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404797-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404797-bib-0001_R_d261997663e518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Funct. Mater. ChemElectroChem J. Mater. Chem. A Nanoscale Horiz. Chem. Rev. Chem. Soc. Rev.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, lithium reserves are limited, and the demand for LIBs has been increasing in recent years, leading to the rising cost of LIBs. Looking for alternatives to lithium has become a hot topic. Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have abundant sodium resources, low cost, and similar working principles to LIBs, making them a promising alternative to lithium-ion batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404797-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404797-bib-0002_R_d261997663e523" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Sci. China Mater. Energy Storage Mater. J. Mater. Chem. A Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Energy Environ. Sci. Chem. Eng. J. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Chem. Soc. Rev.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the energy density of SIBs is lower compared to LIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404797-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404797-bib-0002_R_d261997663e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Sci. China Mater. Energy Storage Mater. J. Mater. Chem. A Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Energy Environ. Sci. Chem. Eng. J. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Chem. Soc. Rev.">2</a>, <a href="#adfm202404797-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404797-bib-0003_R_d261997663e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - J. Mater. Chem. A Mater. Lett. Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To improve the energy density of SIBs, more and more studies focus on the oxygen anion redox (OAR) in SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404797-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404797-bib-0004_R_d261997663e534" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater. Meet. Abstr.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> At a high voltage, oxygen in the lattice also loses electrons, which generates additional capacity beyond the theoretical capacity, greatly improving the energy density of the battery. Therefore, it is very important to study the mechanism of OAR and explore effective strategies to improve the OAR reversibility for high energy density SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>In recent years, some researchers have gained a certain understanding of the triggering mechanism of OAR. The reversible redox activity of oxygen is activated mainly due to the existence of non-bonded O 2<i>p</i> (lO<sub>2</sub><i><sub>p</sub></i>) orbitals. In Li<sub>2</sub>MnO<sub>3</sub>-type structures, one of the O 2<i>p</i>&nbsp;orbitals (the one pointing toward Li in the Li<sub>1/3</sub>M<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>&nbsp;layer) is weakly bonded because of its relatively small overlap with the Li 2<i>s</i>&nbsp;orbital. Hence, it behaves like an O non-bonding state.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404797-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404797-bib-0005_R_d261997663e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy Environ. Sci. Nat. Energy Nat. Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The non-bonding O 2<i>p</i> orbital has high electron energy, and its energy band is above the bonding state (M–O) energy band. Oxygen non-bonding state is the second energy band that can provide charge compensation on the premise of maintaining the basic stability of the structure in addition to the anti-bonding state (M–O)* energy band. This phenomenon has also been reported in SIB cathodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404797-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404797-bib-0006_R_d261997663e571" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Energy Mater. Chem">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> There are two main reasons for the formation of lO<sub>2</sub><i><sub>p</sub></i> orbitals: one is the existence of alkali metal (AM) or alkaline earth metal (AEM) in the transition metal (TM) site (AM/AEM-O-Na), and the other is the vacancy defect in the TM site (Na-O-□).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404797-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404797-bib-0007_R_d261997663e580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. Joule Joule J. Mater. Chem. A Joule Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Mater. Chem. Mater. Electrochem. Commun. Energy Environ. Sci. J. Mater. Sci. Technol. Nat. Sustain.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The special octahedral configuration of oxygen constructs non-bonded electrons in the O 2<i>p</i> orbital, thereby activating reversible OAR. However, in actual experimental testing results, TM oxide cathode materials without special configurations can also undergo reversible OAR. Therefore, the triggering mechanism of OAR has not been fully revealed. Meanwhile, there are still significant challenges in regulating the activity and reversibility of OAR.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we found that in O3-type cathode NaMn<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (MFN), the electronegativity-difference configuration plays a key role on&nbsp;the activation of OAR. Basically, electronegativity is a measure of an atom's ability to attract electrons from atoms bonded to it defined by Pauling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404797-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404797-bib-0008_R_d261997663e599" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - J. Inorg. Nucl. Chem. J. Phys. Chem. A">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The larger the electronegativity is, the atom's ability to attract electrons is stronger. The less the difference in the electronegativity of the atoms, the greater the covalent character of the bond.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404797-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404797-bib-0004_R_d261997663e605" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater. Meet. Abstr.">4</a>, <a href="#adfm202404797-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404797-bib-0009_R_d261997663e605" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nano Energy">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The mechanism of OAR is described as follows. The calculation results of the electron localization function (ELF) show that, when an oxygen ion combines one TM ion with large electronegativity and two TM ions with small electronegativity, it is easy to generate O 2<i>p</i> orbitals with quasi-non-bonded states between O and the TM ion with large electronegativity, thereby activating OAR. Based on this point, we further propose to tune electronegativity-difference configuration with La to construct lO<sub>2</sub><i><sub>p</sub></i> orbitals for reversible OAR in O3-type cathode. This strategy simultaneously improves the OAR activity and reversibility, and achieves a largely enhanced cycle stability under a high voltage (86.9% vs 27.3%@2C@200cycles; 90.8% vs 52.9%@5C@300 cycles). This study reveals the activation and regulation mechanism of OAR in layered oxide cathode materials for SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202404797-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202404797-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202404797-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202404797-sec-0030-title">2.1 Characterization of Structures</h3>
            
            <p>Using TMs Mn, Fe, Ni as matrix and La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> as coating layer source, O3-type cathode materials with different content of La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coating layer were synthesized by high temperature solid-state synthesis (NaMn<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> = MFN, 1wt% La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@NaMn<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> = 1%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN, 2wt% La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@NaMn<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> = 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN, 3wt% La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@NaMn<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> = 3%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN, the synthesis method is given in Supplementary Information). The crystal structures of MFN and the coating layer are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202404797-fig-0001">1a</a>. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404797-fig-0001">1b</a> illustrates the X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns of the MFN and <i>x</i>-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN (x = 1%, 2%, 3%), from which it can be clearly observed that the diffraction peak shapes of the X-ray diffraction of the synthesized four materials are consistent with <i>α</i>-NaFeO<sub>2</sub> crystals, that is, the space group is <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/16ff0289-5099-47b1-b4de-651d0474eee4/adfm202404797-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202404797:adfm202404797-math-0001" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202404797-math-0001.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mrow>
                        <mi>R</mi>
                        <mover accent="true">
                           <mn>3</mn>
                           <mo>¯</mo>
                        </mover>
                        <mi>m</mi>
                     </mrow>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$R{{\bar{3}}}m$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> (No.166). The crystallographic planes corresponding to each diffraction peak have been marked in the figure, and the two highest diffraction peaks correspond to (003) and (104) crystal planes, indicating that the synthesized material is typical of the O3 phase.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202404797-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0ab7bb44-d0fd-4f2b-84a4-89405a0a2e7c/adfm202404797-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/0ab7bb44-d0fd-4f2b-84a4-89405a0a2e7c/adfm202404797-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/742f6450-0ea8-4cf1-b5d9-035c33dfb2a9/adfm202404797-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0ab7bb44-d0fd-4f2b-84a4-89405a0a2e7c/adfm202404797-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202404797-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202404797" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The crystal structure of MFN and La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coating layer. b) The XRD patterns of the prepared MFN and <i>x</i>-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN (<i>x</i> = 1%, 2%, 3%). c) Comparison and analysis of XRD patterns and diffraction information of four groups of prepared materials. d) SEM image, f) TEM image, and h) EDS elemental mapping of MFN. e) SEM image, g) TEM image, and i) EDS elemental mapping of 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN. j) Individual EDS mapping of each element in 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Zooming in on the diffraction peaks located between 16° and 17° (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404797-fig-0001">1c</a>), it can be observed that the diffraction peak corresponding to the (003) crystal plane gradually shifts to the left as the mass fraction of La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> increases from 0% to 2%. When the mass fraction of La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> is 3%, the (003) peak shifts slightly to the right. The location of (003) diffraction peak reflects the information parallel to the <i>d</i>-spacing (TMO<sub>2</sub> layer and Na layer). The left shifting indicates that the <i>d</i>-spacing becomes wider, and the right shifting indicates that the <i>d</i>-spacing becomes narrower. The shifting of (003) peak indicates that the La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coating leads to the diffusion of La<sup>3+</sup> into the bulk of the material, thus increasing the crystal plane spacing of the material. And the crystalline spacing increases gradually with the increase of the coating mass. The (003) diffraction peak of 3%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN is shifted to the right compared to that of 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN, probably because the excess La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> squeezes the lattice and reduces the grain plane spacing or the excess La<sup>3+</sup> changes the original lattice structure. The diffraction peaks of a small number of impurities can also be observed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404797-fig-0001">1c</a>. The peaks marked with # in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404797-fig-0001">1c</a> are present in the XRD patterns of all four materials and are inferred to be the diffraction peaks of NiO (<i>Fm</i>-3<i>m</i>) based on the peak positions. The peak marked with * in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404797-fig-0001">1c</a> is the diffraction peak of La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> (<i>P</i>-3<i>m</i>1). It does not exist in the blank control, and the signal gradually increases with the increase of the mass fraction of La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, which shows that maybe a layer of La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> is coated on the surface of the materials.</p>
            
            <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404797-fig-0001">1d,e</a> shows the morphological structures of MFN and 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN obtained from direct observation by field emission scanning electron microscopy (SEM), respectively. Both are composed of lamellar particles, a morphology often found in typical layered TM oxides. The successful coating of the modified material surface with a thin coating (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404797-fig-0001">1g</a>) relative to the pre-modification (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404797-fig-0001">1f</a>) can be seen in the field emission transmission electron microscopy (TEM) photograph. Furthermore, different regions of SEM to analyze the lattice fringes of the surface coating layer as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). Accordingly, it can be found that the surface is a layer of La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> with a space group of <i>P</i>-3<i>m</i>1. The surface of MFN material is smooth and flat, while there is an uneven coating layer on the surface of 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN material. The internal elemental distribution of the material was observed using field emission transmission electron microscopy-energy dispersive spectrometer (TEM-EDS) analysis to obtain two EDS mapping patterns in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404797-fig-0001">1h,i</a>. Moreover, individual EDS mapping of each element in 2%-La2O3@MFN in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404797-fig-0001">1j</a>. It can be confirmed that the surface coating layer contains a large amount of La elements. Combined with the results of XRD, it can be determined that the surface of the 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN material is coated with a layer of La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coating. Moreover, it can also be confirmed that several elements, O, Na, Mn, Fe, and Ni, are uniformly distributed in the material without obvious biased impurity phases.</p>
            
            <p>To determine the exact material composition, it is necessary to detect the exact content of TMs using inductively coupled plasma mass spectrometry (ICP-MS), the results of which are shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). From the results obtained, the actual content of Na, Mn, Fe, and Ni agree with the chemical formula within the error allowed. In addition, the results show that the main substitution of La doped into the lattice is Fe. After determining the elemental composition, it is necessary to clarify the elemental valence states within the material. The results obtained by powder X-ray photoelectronic spectrometer (XPS) are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). The peak shape has been fitted. The fitting results show that Mn in the material mainly presents +4 valence and Ni mainly presents +2 valence.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404797-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404797-bib-0010_R_d261997663e904" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nanoscale">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, there is a higher relative content of Mn<sup>4+</sup> and Ni<sup>2+</sup> within the modified material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404797-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404797-bib-0011_R_d261997663e914" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Colloids Surf. A">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The multiplet splitting energy of La's 3<i>d</i><sub>5/2</sub> peak is 4.36&nbsp;eV, indicating that La exists in the form of La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404797-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404797-bib-0012_R_d261997663e927" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202404797-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202404797-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p>The electrochemical performance of MFN and <i>x</i>-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN (<i>x</i> = 1%, 2%, and 3%) was tested by assembling half-cells (coin cells 2025) in a voltage window of 1.5–4.3&nbsp;V. By comprehensively comparing the rate performance and cycle performance, the material with 2% mass fraction La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coating has the best performance. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information) shows the first constant current charge/discharge curves for MFN and <i>x</i>-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN (<i>x</i> = 1%, 2%, and 3%) cathodes at 0.05 C with initial discharge capacities of 182, 179, 172, and 171 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. In the process of coating sintering, La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coating will cause La<sup>3+</sup> to diffuse into the lattice to replace the TM, resulting in the attenuation of capacity. Therefore, with the increase of coating amount, there will be a slight loss of capacity. Several plateaus can be seen in the first charge/discharge curves. The platforms below 4.0&nbsp;V reflect the phase change caused by TM oxide layer sliding. In this part, TMs participate in oxidation and reduction to provide capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404797-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404797-bib-0013_R_d261997663e973" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Appl. Therm. Eng. J. Mater. Chem. A Energy Environ. Sci. Nat. Mater. J. Electrochem. Soc.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition to the capacity provided by TM ions, O<sup>2−</sup> will also provide capacity during charge and discharge. The platform above 4.0&nbsp;V is the phase transition caused by O<sup>2−</sup> participating in the oxidation reaction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404797-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404797-bib-0014_R_d261997663e983" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nat. Commun.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is found that with the increase of La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coating mass fraction, the platform will become longer, and the capacity provided by oxygen electron loss will be greater. This phenomenon shows that La<sup>3+</sup> may stimulate the redox activity of oxygen.</p>
            
            <p><b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202404797-fig-0002">2a</a> shows the rate performance of MFN and <i>x</i>-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN (<i>x</i> = 1%, 2%, and 3%) cathodes. 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN cathodes can provide high reversible capacities of 172, 152, 138, 122, 109, 96, and 80 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at different current densities of 0.05 C, 0.1 C, 0.2 C, 0.5 C, 1 C, 2 C, and 5 C, respectively. The high reversible capacities of 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN are still 51 and 33 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> even at high rate of 8 C and 10 C, while the capacity of MFN cathode under the same conditions is less than 2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202404797-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/449b7ad2-4e62-4460-a634-f660048af0a9/adfm202404797-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/449b7ad2-4e62-4460-a634-f660048af0a9/adfm202404797-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6c5435e8-b6b8-4021-b14e-af1d4541b509/adfm202404797-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/449b7ad2-4e62-4460-a634-f660048af0a9/adfm202404797-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202404797-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202404797" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Rate performance of MFN and <i>x</i>-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN (<i>x</i>  =  1%, 2%, and 3%) at various current densities. b) Cycling performance of MFN and 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN at 2 C in the voltage of 1.5–4.3 V. c) Cycling performance of MFN and 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN at 5 C in the voltage of 1.5–4.3 V. Cyclic voltammograms of d) MFN and e) 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN cathodes in the potential range of 1.5–4.3&nbsp;V.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information) and Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404797-fig-0002">2b,c</a> show the cycling performance results for the MFN and <i>x</i>-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN (<i>x</i> = 1%, 2% and 3%) cathodes at different current densities. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information) shows the cycle performance of MFN and <i>x</i>-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN (<i>x</i> = 1%, 2% and 3%) cathodes after 50 cycles at 0.1 C, the capacity retention rate of MFN is only 56%, while 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN is 83%. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information) shows the cycle performance of MFN and <i>x</i>-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN (<i>x</i> = 1%, 2% and 3%) cathodes after 100 cycles at 1 C, the capacity retention rate of MFN is only 41%, while 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN is 81%. After 200 cycles at 2 C for MFN and 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN two cathodes are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404797-fig-0002">2b</a>, the 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN cathode still has a capacity of about 99 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, and the capacity retention rate has increased from 27% to 87%. At a current density of 5 C, 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN material shows excellent high capacity and cycle stability. The initial capacity is about 85 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, and the capacity retention rate remains above 91% after 300 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404797-fig-0002">2c</a>). Under the same conditions, the capacity retention of MFN is less than 53%. The electrochemical performance data of the modified MFN reported in the literatures are compared with our 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN, as shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). It could be found that 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN exhibits superior electrochemical performance, indicating its enormous potential for industrial applications.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404797-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404797-bib-0015_R_d261997663e1157" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Chem. Mater. J. Alloys Compd. J. Energy Storage J. Power Sources Sci. China Mater. Solid State Sci.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>Cyclic voltammetry (CV) experiments were performed to investigate the electrochemical behavior of MFN and 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404797-fig-0002">2d,e</a> shows the CV curves for MFN and 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN in the scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> in the range of 1.5-4.3&nbsp;V, respectively. The CV curves of both materials have similar shapes, indicating that the redox reactions occurring in the materials before and after the modification are basically the same. The lower voltage Peaks 1 and 4 are generated by the redox reaction of TMs, while the higher voltage Peaks 2 and 3 are generated by the redox reaction of O. The comparison of the CV curves of the two materials shows that the same peak voltage difference value between the second and sixth cycles of 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN is smaller than that of MFN, and the voltage decay rate is smaller than that of the MFN, indicating that the structure of the electrode material is more stable after coating. The voltage difference value between the oxidation peak and reduction peak reflects the degree of polarization. The voltage difference value between oxidation peaks and reduction peaks of 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN is smaller than that of the MFN, indicating that the degree of polarization of the electrode material is reduced after coating. In addition, with the increase of the number of cycles, each redox peak of the blank sample decreased significantly more than that of the modified sample, indicating that the redox reversibility of TM and oxygen in the modified sample was enhanced. CV results show that the reversibility and stability of redox reaction of oxygen and TM are enhanced after coating La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, and the polarization of each redox peak becomes smaller.</p>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient in the cathode can be calculated based on the CV test results with the help of the potentiostatic intermittent titration technique (PITT) test results. The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient can be calculated by the following equation<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404797-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404797-bib-0016_R_d261997663e1199" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Power Sources J. Mater. Chem. A">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>:

               <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202404797-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/a8e2dde1-4e22-4453-839d-75531b6f1959/adfm202404797-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202404797:adfm202404797-math-0002" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202404797-math-0002.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>I</mi>
                                 <mi>p</mi>
                              </msub>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                              <mn>2.69</mn>
                              <mo linebreak="goodbreak">×</mo>
                              <msup>
                                 <mn>10</mn>
                                 <mn>5</mn>
                              </msup>
                              <msup>
                                 <mi>n</mi>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mn>3</mn>
                                    <mo>/</mo>
                                    <mn>2</mn>
                                 </mrow>
                              </msup>
                              <mi>A</mi>
                              <msup>
                                 <mi>D</mi>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mn>1</mn>
                                    <mo>/</mo>
                                    <mn>2</mn>
                                 </mrow>
                              </msup>
                              <msup>
                                 <mi>v</mi>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mn>1</mn>
                                    <mo>/</mo>
                                    <mn>2</mn>
                                 </mrow>
                              </msup>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>C</mi>
                                 <mn>0</mn>
                              </msub>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{{I}_p} = 2.69 \times {{10}^5}{{n}^{3/2}}A{{D}^{1/2}}{{v}^{1/2}}\Delta {{C}_0}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>where <i>I<sub>p</sub></i> is the peak current, <i>n</i> is the number of electrons transferred in the redox reaction (1 for Na<sup>+</sup>), <i>A</i> is the area entering the cathode in the electrolyte, <i>D</i> is the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient, Δ<i>C<sub>0</sub></i> is the concentration of Na<sup>+</sup> change, and <i>v</i> is the scan speed. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information) shows the PITT test results for MFN and 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN cathode. The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient of 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN is higher than MFN in the discharging process.</div>
            
            <p>Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information) shows the electrochemical impedance spectra (EIS) of the MFN and 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN cathode. The <i>R<sub>p</sub></i> of 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN is 524.90 Ω and <i>R<sub>s</sub></i> is 10.78 Ω, which is significantly less than 823.82 Ω and 14.38 Ω of MFN, indicating that the uneven La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coating will not increase the surface resistance of the material, on the contrary, it may inhibit the precipitation of carbon oxides of Na on the surface. In addition, the results show that the resistance to Na<sup>+</sup> migration in the 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN cathode is smaller, which is consistent with the conclusion of the diffusion coefficient obtained above.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202404797-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202404797-sec-0050-title">2.3 Modification Mechanism of Lattice Regulation and Surface Coating</h3>
            
            <p>To obtain the crystal structure information of the materials, the materials were performed by neutron powder diffraction (NPD) and XRD. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202404797-fig-0003">3a,b</a> shows MFN and 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN NPD data with Rietveld refinement by software Fullprof, respectively, and the structural data of the material before and after modification are shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information). All data <i>R<sub>p</sub></i> does not exceed 4% and <i>R<sub>wp</sub></i> does not exceed 5%, indicating that the obtained data are within the error tolerance. The atomic occupation information of MFN and 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN are shown in Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information), respectively. 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN has the smaller lattice parameter <i>a</i> = 2.9802 Å and bigger parameter <i>c</i> = 16.0151 Å for the two materials, while the Na layer width is the larger (3.6464 Å) and the TM oxide layer width is the smaller (1.7836 Å). A wider Na layer can ensure that Na ions can migrate more smoothly, and a narrower TM layer can ensure that the material skeleton is more stable. The corresponding ones are the longer Na─O bond length (2.3615 Å) and the shorter TM─O bond length (2.0166 Å). The increase of Na─O bond length decreases the Coulomb force between Na<sup>+</sup> and O<sup>2−</sup>, and the potential barrier of migration is reduced, which is favorable to the migration of Na<sup>+</sup>. The reduced TM─O bond length increases the Coulomb force between TM ion and O<sup>2−</sup>, which increases the lattice energy and thus enhances the lattice stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404797-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404797-bib-0017_R_d261997663e1379" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Mater. Chem. Phys.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the modified material has excellent rate performance and cycle stability. Moreover, the improvement of PITT and EIS results is also possible due to the regulation of lattice structure by La<sup>3+</sup> doping, which widens the sodium layer and lengthens the Na─O bond.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202404797-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4e1800dd-6917-4d08-af6e-fa3f4d276e4e/adfm202404797-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/4e1800dd-6917-4d08-af6e-fa3f4d276e4e/adfm202404797-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/20a4ed8c-168c-4e43-bc76-596258d69dd3/adfm202404797-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4e1800dd-6917-4d08-af6e-fa3f4d276e4e/adfm202404797-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202404797-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202404797" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Refinement results of the NPD of a) MFN and b) 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN. c) Schematic diagram of MFN and 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN crystal structure drawn according to neutron diffraction refinement, and Mn─O, Fe─O, Ni─O, and La─O octahedrons and bond energy. In situ XRD picture of d) MFN and e) 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN. f) Ex situ soft-XAS of Mn L-edge of MFN and 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN in TEY mode. g) Ex situ soft-XAS of Ni L-edge of MFN and 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN in TEY mode.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information) shows the X-ray diffraction refinement results for the MFN and <i>x</i>-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN (<i>x</i> = 1%, 2%, 3%) after Rietveld refinement with software Fullprof, and the relevant data calculated from the refinement results are shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information). The two comparisons confirm the conclusions obtained from the NPD patterns, and the data given in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information) can be considered reliable. XRD refinement results show that after coating 2%&nbsp;wt La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, the material can obtain the largest Na layer spacing and the narrowest TMO<sub>2</sub> layer.</p>
            
            <p>The schematic diagram of MFN and 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN crystal structure is drawn according to neutron diffraction refinement, using the software VESTA, shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404797-fig-0003">3c</a>. The TM oxide layer and Na layer are arranged in an ABCABC manner, which is a typical O3 phase structure, and the La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coating makes the thickness of the TM oxide layer decrease and the thickness of the Na layer increase. Moreover, La─O bond energy is much greater than other bond energies, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404797-fig-0003">3c</a>. The strong La─O bond energy (as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information) is one of the important reasons for&nbsp;stabilizing the material structure and inhibiting the phase transition.</p>
            
            <p>To further study the effect of lattice regulation by La doping during charge and discharge, the materials were tested by in situ and ex situ XRD, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404797-fig-0003">3d,e</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10a,b</a> (Supporting Information). The in situ XRD experimental results are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404797-fig-0003">3d,e</a>. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404797-fig-0003">3d</a> indicates that the bare sample undergoes a phase transition of O3 → P3 → O3’+P3 during the charging process, and O3’+P3 → O3+P3 during the discharging process. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404797-fig-0003">3e</a> indicates that the modified sample undergoes a phase transition of O3 → P3 → O3’ during charging to 4.3&nbsp;V. During the process of discharging to 1.5&nbsp;V, the modified sample undergoes a reversible phase transition from O3’ to P3 to O3. This indicates that La doping enhances the reversibility of the structure. The ex situ XRD results show that both the electrode materials change from the original O3 phase to the P3 phase during the charging process, and then the opposite process occurs with the discharge (2.7&nbsp;V → 4.1&nbsp;V → 1.5&nbsp;V). This conforms to the phase transformation law of ordinary O3-type materials. However, there are differences in the phase transformation process between the modified and unmodified materials. Comparing Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10c</a> (Supporting Information) with Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10d</a> (Supporting Information), it can be found that when charging to 4.1&nbsp;V, the intensity of P3 (003) peak in MFN sample is higher than that of modified sample, even higher than the initial state, and the peak half height width is also narrower. This indicates that TM ions migrate to the Na layer in the bare sample during the phase transition. This phenomenon is not obvious in the modified samples. In addition, the (003) peak position of the MFN is shifted from 16.48° to as far as 15.63° and then back to 16.41°, while the (003) peak position of 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN is shifted from 16.44° to as far as 15.59° and then back to 16.42°. The maximum shift is the same, but the gap between the peak positions is smaller after coating La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, which means that the structure of the cathode material after coating has fewer structural changes before and after charging–discharging compared with the MFN. In the modified samples, due to the lattice regulation of La<sup>3+</sup> doping, the migration of TM ions is well suppressed, which thanks to the strong stability of the TM layer caused by the strong bond energy La–O octahedron and short TM─O bond. Therefore, the structural strength and reversibility of the modified sample is stronger during charge and discharge. This is one of the reasons why the coated material can maintain stability under long cycle.</p>
            
            <p>La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coating could protect the completeness of the material surface and avoid the reduction and dissolution of high valence TM ions (Mn<sup>4+</sup>, Ni<sup>3+</sup>, Ni<sup>4+</sup> etc.) by electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404797-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404797-bib-0018_R_d261997663e1526" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Electrochem. Soc. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the process of cycle, high valence manganese ions are easy to be reduced to Mn<sup>3+</sup>, or even Mn<sup>2+</sup>. In addition, the disproportionation reaction of Mn<sup>3+</sup> will promote the dissolution of Mn (Mn<sup>4+</sup> + e<sup>−</sup> → Mn<sup>3+</sup>, 2Mn<sup>3+</sup> → Mn<sup>4+</sup> + Mn<sup>2+</sup>). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404797-fig-0003">3f</a>, ex situ soft X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) of Mn L-edge of MFN and 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN were collected in TEY mode. Total electron yield (TEY) mode mainly detects the depth of 10&nbsp;nm on the particle surface, which reflects the information of the surface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404797-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404797-bib-0019_R_d261997663e1558" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the initial state, only a few Mn ions gather on the material surface, so the collected signal is weak. After the start of charging, the TM ions on the outermost surface are dissolved in the electrolyte. At this time, the signal of surface Mn ions can be detected. When charged to 4.0&nbsp;V, Mn ions on the material surface mainly exist in the form of Mn<sup>2+</sup>, accompanied by a small amount of Mn<sup>3+</sup> and Mn<sup>4+</sup>. When charged to 4.3&nbsp;V, Mn<sup>2+</sup> and Mn<sup>3+</sup> will be oxidized to Mn<sup>4+</sup>. When discharged to 1.5&nbsp;V, Mn<sup>2+</sup>, Mn<sup>3+</sup>, and Mn<sup>4+</sup> will exist on the material surface, of which Mn<sup>2+</sup> content is the most. Compare MFN to 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN, during the whole charging and discharging process, the content of Mn<sup>2+</sup> and Mn<sup>3+</sup> on the 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN material surface is significantly less than that of MFN, because La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coating protects the material surface and inhibits the dissolution of Mn<sup>4+</sup>. Similarly, the average valence state of Ni ions on the material surface is between +2 and +3 valence (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404797-fig-0003">3g</a>). After one cycle of charge and discharge, compared with 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN, Ni ions on the surface of MFN material are reduced to a lower valence state +2. Without the protection of La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, the structure of the material surface becomes unstable, which will significantly reduce the stability of the material in the long cycle process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404797-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404797-bib-0020_R_d261997663e1616" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Energy Storage Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>The surface morphology of the material was observed by SEM after 200 cycles of charge/discharge at 1 C, and the results are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information). Multiple deep cracks could be found on the flake particles of the MFN after cycling. Cracks can also be found on the 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN material particles, but they are relatively shallow. There is a view that the cracks are caused by the erosion of the material surface by electrolyte and the slip of the layer caused by irreversible phase transformation. The results show that the sample coated with La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> can well inhibit the above two points and keep the material structure relatively intact. The lattice parameters of MFN and 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN cathode materials after 300 cycles at 5 C are shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information), which are obtained by XRD refinement. The results show that the lattice change of the modified material decreases obviously after long cycles, and the cycle stability is stronger.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202404797-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202404797-sec-0060-title">2.4 Oxygen Anion Redox Chemistry</h3>
            
            <p>Activating and stabilizing the oxidation–reduction of oxygen under high voltage is of great significance in improving the working voltage platform and increasing the energy density of SIBs. Initial galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of MFN and 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN at 0.05 C in the voltage window of 1.5–4.3 V are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202404797-fig-0004">4a</a>. In the charging process, the MFN sample can provide the capacity of 45 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> from OAR, while the modified sample can provide 52 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, which makes up for the capacity attenuation problem after La<sup>3+</sup> replaces TM ions to a certain extent and improves the working voltage platform.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202404797-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/02514b43-5374-4117-870c-fa1192ecb57e/adfm202404797-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/02514b43-5374-4117-870c-fa1192ecb57e/adfm202404797-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/24fb527e-1203-420c-868b-7fc60701a1dd/adfm202404797-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/02514b43-5374-4117-870c-fa1192ecb57e/adfm202404797-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202404797-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202404797" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Initial galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of MFN and 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN at 0.05 C in the voltage window of 1.5–4.3 V. b) Ex situ soft-XAS of O K-edge of MFN and 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN in FY mode. c–e) Valence band spectra of different charge and discharge states for MFN and 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN. f–h) O 1<i>s</i> XPS spectra of different charge and discharge states for MFN and 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To study the redox mechanism of oxygen in MFN and 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN, ex situ soft XAS of two materials were collected in the O K-edge region (FY mode), as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404797-fig-0004">4b</a>. The test depth of fluorescence yield (FY) mode can reach 50&nbsp;nm, which reflects the information of bulk phase.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404797-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404797-bib-0019_R_d261997663e1717" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404797-fig-0004">4b</a>, the peak before 535&nbsp;eV reflects the information of M 3<i>d</i>-O 2<i>p</i> hybrid orbit (pink region), and the peak after 535&nbsp;eV reflects the information of M 4<i>sp</i>-O 2<i>p</i> hybrid orbit (yellow region). The 3<i>d</i> band pre-edge peak at ≈531&nbsp;eV (orange line) shifts to lower energy when charging to 4.3&nbsp;V, which is attributed to the oxidation of TM ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404797-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404797-bib-0021_R_d261997663e1737" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The integrated intensity of low energy peaks (before 535&nbsp;eV) with different charge states can supply important information about the unoccupied hole states of the O 2<i>p</i> orbitals, which was previously verified with soft XAS by Bruce et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404797-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404797-bib-0022_R_d261997663e1744" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nat. Chem. Phys. Rev. B">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The integrated intensity of the pre-edge peak is calculated between 528 and 535&nbsp;eV, and the results are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> (Supporting Information). The integral area results show that OAR will occur under high voltage in two samples, and La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN has stronger oxygen redox activity and stronger reversibility than MFN, which is consistent with the results of charge/discharge and CV curves.</p>
            
            <p>Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13a–f</a> (Supporting Information) shows ex situ XPS results of MFN and 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN in O 1<i>s</i> energy region after etching 15&nbsp;nm. The results show a new peak of O<sup>−</sup>/(O<sub>2</sub>)<sup>2−</sup> at about 530.3&nbsp;eV appears when charged to 4.3&nbsp;V, indicating that oxygen participates in redox. The results of XPS can well support the results of XAS.</p>
            
            <p>To further study the oxidation–reduction phenomenon of oxygen in before and after modification samples, valence band spectra and XPS spectra of different elements in different etching depths were collected (etching speed: 6&nbsp;nm&nbsp;min<sup>−1</sup>). Valence band spectra of different charge and discharge states are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404797-fig-0004">4c,e</a>. The results show that two materials show a consistent law from the surface to the bulk. When charging to 4.3&nbsp;V, a new energy band (blue region) appears in the range of 0–5&nbsp;eV below the Fermi level, which is formed by the electron transfer from O orbit to the TM orbit, which is a reduction coupling phenomenon. This proves that oxygen loses electrons under high voltage. And the results show that the reduction coupling phenomenon of the modified sample is more obvious, the strength of energy band is higher, indicating that the oxygen activity is higher.</p>
            
            <p>O 1<i>s</i> XPS spectra of different charge and discharge states are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404797-fig-0004">4f–h</a>. When charging to 4.3&nbsp;V, the O 1<i>s</i> spectrum of the modified sample shifts to the high energy region, indicating that the overall valence state of oxygen is higher and the degree of oxygen participating in the reaction is higher. Mn 2<i>p</i> XPS spectra of different charge and discharge states are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14a–c</a> (Supporting Information). When charging to the highest voltage (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14b</a>, Supporting Information), it can be clearly observed that the modified sample shifts to lower energy, which is caused by the reduction coupling phenomenon, which proves that the oxygen activity of the modified sample is stronger. The same result also appears in Ni (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14h</a>, Supporting Information) but is not obvious in Fe (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14e</a>, Supporting Information). It shows that electrons from O 2<i>p</i> tend to transfer to the 3<i>d</i> orbits of Mn and Ni. In conclusion, the redox reaction of oxygen will occur in MFN system, and the doping of La element will activate the activity of oxygen.</p>
            
            <p>Yang et&nbsp;al. pointed out that using O-K edge soft XAS to characterize the oxidation–reduction of oxygen ions is not rigorous.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404797-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404797-bib-0023_R_d261997663e1817" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Power Sources">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the resonant inelastic X-ray scattering (RIXS) experiments were conducted to characterize the OAR of MFN and 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN materials, which can support the results of soft XAS. RIXS is a technology with a detection depth of about 100&nbsp;nm within the O K-edge range, which is sensitive to the oxidation state of oxygen and can characterize the redox process of lattice oxygen.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404797-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404797-bib-0024_R_d261997663e1826" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Nat. Energy">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The ex situ O K-edge RIXS results of MFN and 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202404797-fig-0005">5a,b</a>, respectively. The incident energy of RIXS is 531&nbsp;eV, corresponding to the elastic peak at 0&nbsp;eV energy loss. The RIXS energy loss spectrum can be divided into four regions: (A) elastic peak region, (B) O–O dimer oscillation peak region, (C) electronic structure region of metal oxide d band, which can reflect the octahedron information of TM-O hybridization, and (D) information region of O 2<i>p</i> orbital electrons. The peak of approximately 523.5&nbsp;eV in the D region (corresponding to an energy loss of 7.5&nbsp;eV) is the fingerprint peak of the oxygen oxidation reaction caused by electron loss in O 2<i>p</i>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404797-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404797-bib-0007_R_d261997663e1847" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. Joule Joule J. Mater. Chem. A Joule Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Mater. Chem. Mater. Electrochem. Commun. Energy Environ. Sci. J. Mater. Sci. Technol. Nat. Sustain.">7</a>, <a href="#adfm202404797-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404797-bib-0025_R_d261997663e1847" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nat. Commun.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the MFN and 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN samples, when charged to 4.3&nbsp;V, in D region, a fingerprint peak appeared, indicating that OAR occurred in both groups of materials, and the peak intensity of 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN was stronger than that of MFN, indicating that the activity of oxygen ions in 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN was higher, which is consistent with the results of soft XAS.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202404797-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/fafb344d-dd21-49b0-8d08-c5c8901bd958/adfm202404797-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/fafb344d-dd21-49b0-8d08-c5c8901bd958/adfm202404797-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d66a5e85-ab96-46a5-9b98-85305e95c4dd/adfm202404797-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/fafb344d-dd21-49b0-8d08-c5c8901bd958/adfm202404797-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202404797-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202404797" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Ex situ O K-edge RIXS results for a) MFN and b) 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN. c,d) are enlarged views of region B in (a,b). DEMS results of e) MFN and f) 2%-La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN materials.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Region B reflects the frequency information of vibrations between adjacent oxygen atoms caused by energy loss and can determine the morphology of dimers formed by two oxygen atoms. The O–O vibration frequency of molecular oxygen O<sub>2</sub> is about 1556 cm<sup>−1</sup>, and the O–O vibration frequency of peroxide (O<sub>2</sub>)<sup>2−</sup> and superoxide (O<sub>2</sub>)<sup>−</sup> is about 743 and 1108 cm<sup>−1</sup>, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404797-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404797-bib-0026_R_d261997663e1911" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Nature">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404797-fig-0005">5c,d</a> are enlarged views of the B region in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404797-fig-0005">5a,b</a>, respectively. The RIXS results of the B region show that both groups of samples exhibited vibration signals when charged to 4.3&nbsp;V, forming molecular-oxygen-like oxygen dimers (with oscillation frequencies of around 1450 cm<sup>−1</sup>). The vibration intensity of the modified sample is greater than that of the bare sample, indicating a higher degree of oxygen participation in the reaction. When discharged to 1.5&nbsp;V, the oscillation signal of the modified sample disappears, indicating that OAR is reversible. However, there are still oscillating peaks in the bare sample, indicating that the OAR reaction is irreversible.</p>
            
            <p>To determine the state of molecular oxygen generation in the material at high voltage, differential electrochemical mass spectrometry (DEMS) experiments were conducted on the materials to detect whether molecular oxygen is bonded within the material or escaped in the form of oxygen molecules. The experiment results are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404797-fig-0005">5e,f</a>. The results show that neither group of materials produces oxygen gas, which is consistent with previous reports.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404797-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404797-bib-0026_R_d261997663e1931" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Nature">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Below 4.0&nbsp;V, the small amount of CO<sub>2</sub> generated by the two groups of samples is related to the decomposition of the electrolyte. However, at a high voltage above 4.0&nbsp;V, the MFN sample generated a large amount of CO<sub>2</sub> gas, which is due to the chemical reaction between the generated molecular oxygen and the electrolyte. While the CO<sub>2</sub> gas evolution is suppressed in the modified sample. The results indicate that OAR is irreversible in MFN.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404797-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404797-bib-0027_R_d261997663e1943" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Electrochem. Soc. Adv. Funct. Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Based on the results of RIXS, it can be concluded that the La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coating layer hinders the direct contact of the material with the electrolyte, thereby inhibiting the reaction between molecular oxygen and the electrolyte, making OAR reversible. In the bare sample, without the protection of the coating layer, the molecular oxygen on the surface of the material directly reacts with the electrolyte continuously to produce CO<sub>2</sub>, so OAR is irreversible and the molecular oxygen signal can still be detected in the discharge state.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202404797-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202404797-sec-0070-title">2.5 Theoretical Calculation</h3>
            
            <p>To reveal the redox mechanism of oxygen, we thus decided to perform density functional theory (DFT) calculations. In SIBs, the electrons involved in the reversible redox reaction of oxygen mainly come from the lO<sub>2</sub><i><sub>p</sub></i> orbital. lO<sub>2</sub><i><sub>p</sub></i> lone pairs electrons orbital can be visualized (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202404797-fig-0006">6a,b</a>) from the ELF calculation using a high ELF iso-surface value of 0.65&nbsp;eV. Two lobes can easily be distinguished in the model compound MFN and La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404797-fig-0006">6a</a> dashed circle area, one big lobe corresponding to the non-bonded O 2<i>s</i> state (lO<sub>2</sub><i><sub>s</sub></i>) and another small one pointing along the Ni─O or Fe─O bond indicative of the quasi-non-bonded O 2<i>p</i> state.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404797-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404797-bib-0028_R_d261997663e1995" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Energy Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In MFN system, one oxygen atom will bond three TM atoms, and there are 10 different bonding modes. The calculation results show that only in the bonding mode of Fe-O-Mn<sub>2</sub> and Ni-O-Mn<sub>2</sub>, there will be non-bonded O 2<i>p</i> states pointing to the direction of Fe─O or Ni─O bond.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202404797-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b120688f-aa37-4f48-b294-4c58263f9adb/adfm202404797-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/b120688f-aa37-4f48-b294-4c58263f9adb/adfm202404797-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fee2d885-d03e-432b-a1f1-6688d060cef5/adfm202404797-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b120688f-aa37-4f48-b294-4c58263f9adb/adfm202404797-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202404797-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202404797" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">ELF iso-surface for high value (0.65&nbsp;eV) of a) MFN and b) La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN high ELF value reflects a high concentration of paired localized electrons. If the basin is centered on 1 atom, the electrons are nonbonding. The two lobes around each oxygen correspond to lO<sub>2</sub><i><sub>s</sub></i> and the lO<sub>2</sub><i><sub>p</sub></i> lone pair states. ELF iso-surface for medium value (0.046&nbsp;eV) of c) MFN and d) La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN. In contrast: a local basin shared between two atoms at medium ELF value indicates the occurrence of covalent bonds between these two atoms. The calculation results of pDOS of e) MFN and f) La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@MFN. g) Schematic diagram of TM-O covalent molecular bond. h) Schematic diagram of TM-O ionic molecular bond. i) Schematic diagram of La-O ionic molecular bond.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In the TM oxygen octahedral hybrid structure, TM-O molecular bonds exhibit both covalent and ionic properties. When the difference in electronegativity between O atom and TM atom is small, TM-O bond will express more covalence, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404797-fig-0006">6g</a>. The electron cloud around O atom and TM atom is close to and overlaps, and electrons share and recombine. O atom and TM atom share electrons to form covalent molecular bonds. Due to the presence of two non-bonded electron pairs in the O atom itself (lO<sub>2</sub><i><sub>s</sub></i> and lO<sub>2</sub><i><sub>p</sub></i>), in this case, after the O atom forms a covalent bond with the TM atom, it is still possible to retain these two non-bonded electron pairs because they do not form additional covalent bonds with the electrons in the metal atom. Therefore, after orbital hybridization, theoretically there will be electrons of lO<sub>2</sub><i><sub>p</sub></i> orbitals.</p>
